   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Emoticon</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub">
     <div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint">
      <div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic">
       <span aria-disabled="false" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-articleSearch oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-iconWidget"></span><span id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" aria-disabled="false" title="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicator-down oo-ui-indicatorElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-indicatorWidget"></span>
      </div>

    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Pictorial representation of a facial expression using punctuation marks, numbers and letters
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Emoji" title="Emoji">Emoji</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sticker_(messaging)" title="Sticker (messaging)">Sticker (messaging)</a>.
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(technical_restrictions)#Semicolons" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (technical restrictions)">technical reasons</a>, search terms like ";;" redirect here.
      </div> 
      <p class="mw-empty-elt"> </p> 
      <table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 17em;"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Emoji_u263a.svg" title="File:Emoji u263a.svg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Emoji_u263a.svg/40px-Emoji_u263a.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Emoji_u263a.svg/60px-Emoji_u263a.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Emoji_u263a.svg/80px-Emoji_u263a.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128"></a></td> 
         <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Emoji#Unicode_blocks" title="Emoji">Unicode emoticons or emojis</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of the intended characters.</td>
        </tr> 
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Emoticon_Smile_Face.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Emoticon_Smile_Face.svg/180px-Emoticon_Smile_Face.svg.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Emoticon_Smile_Face.svg/270px-Emoticon_Smile_Face.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Emoticon_Smile_Face.svg/360px-Emoticon_Smile_Face.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="295" data-file-height="295"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Emoticon_Smile_Face.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A <a href="/wiki/Smiley-face" class="mw-redirect" title="Smiley-face">smiley-face</a> emoticon
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>An <b>emoticon</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/: 'i' in 'kit'">?</span><span title="/?/: primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/o?/: 'o' in 'code'">o?</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/?/: 'i' in 'kit'">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/?/: 'o' in 'body'">?</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">?-<span style="font-size:90%">MOH</span>-t?-kon</i></a>, rarely pronounced <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/: 'i' in 'kit'">?</span><span title="/?/: primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/?/: 'o' in 'body'">?</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/?/: 'i' in 'kit'">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/?/: 'o' in 'body'">?</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> short for "emotion icon",<sup id="cite_ref-Education_2003_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Education_2003-5">[5]</a></sup> also known simply as an <b>emote</b>, is a pictorial representation of a <a href="/wiki/Facial_expression" title="Facial expression">facial expression</a> using <a href="/wiki/Character_(symbol)" title="Character (symbol)">characters</a>—usually <a href="/wiki/Punctuation_mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Punctuation mark">punctuation marks</a>, numbers, and letters—to express a person's feelings, mood or reaction, or as a time-saving method. </p>
      <p>Early emoticons were the precursors to modern emojis, which are ever-developing predominantly on iOS and Android devices. The first ASCII emoticons, <code>:-)</code> and <code>:-(</code>, were written by <a href="/wiki/Scott_Fahlman" title="Scott Fahlman">Scott Fahlman</a> in 1982, but emoticons actually originated on the <a href="/wiki/PLATO_(computer_system)" title="PLATO (computer system)">PLATO IV</a> computer system in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Kaomoji.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kaomoji.png/180px-Kaomoji.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="76" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kaomoji.png/270px-Kaomoji.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/Kaomoji.png 2x" data-file-width="358" data-file-height="151"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Kaomoji.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Examples of kaomoji smileys
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In Western countries, emoticons are usually written at a right angle to the direction of the text. Users from <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> popularized a kind of emoticon called <b>kaomoji</b>, utilizing the <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">Katakana</a> character set, that can be understood without tilting one's head to the left. This style arose on ASCII NET of Japan in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-aist_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aist-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-whatjapanthinks2007_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatjapanthinks2007-8">[8]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As <a href="/wiki/SMS" title="SMS">SMS</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> became widespread in the late 1990s, emoticons became increasingly popular and were commonly used in text messages, <a href="/wiki/Internet_forum" title="Internet forum">Internet forums</a> and <a href="/wiki/Email" title="Email">e-mails</a>. Emoticons have played a significant role in communication through technology, and some devices and applications have provided stylized pictures that do not use text punctuation. They offer another range of "tone" and feeling through texting that portrays specific emotions through facial gestures while in the midst of text-based cyber communication.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-emoticon">Pre-emoticon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-emoticon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Emoticons began with the suggestion that combinations of punctuation could be used in typography to indicate emotion. While <a href="/wiki/Scott_Fahlman" title="Scott Fahlman">Scott Fahlman</a>'s suggestion in the 1980s was the birth of the emoticon, it wasn't the first occasion that&nbsp;:) or&nbsp;:-) was used in text. </p>
      <p>In 1648, poet <a href="/wiki/Robert_Herrick_(poet)" title="Robert Herrick (poet)">Robert Herrick</a> included the lines: </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <div class="poem"> 
        <p>Tumble me down, and I will sit<br> Upon my ruins, (smiling yet:) </p> 
       </div>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Herrick's work predated any other recorded use of brackets as a smiling face by around 200 years. However, experts have since weighed whether the inclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Colon_(punctuation)" title="Colon (punctuation)">colon</a> in the poem was deliberate and if it was meant to represent a smiling face. English professor Alan Jacobs argued "punctuation, in general, was unsettled in the seventeenth century... Herrick was unlikely to have consistent punctuational practices himself, and even if he did he couldn't expect either his printers or his readers to share them."<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Alleged_use_of_emoticon,_1862-08-07.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Alleged_use_of_emoticon%2C_1862-08-07.jpg/220px-Alleged_use_of_emoticon%2C_1862-08-07.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Alleged_use_of_emoticon%2C_1862-08-07.jpg/330px-Alleged_use_of_emoticon%2C_1862-08-07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Alleged_use_of_emoticon%2C_1862-08-07.jpg/440px-Alleged_use_of_emoticon%2C_1862-08-07.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="210"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Alleged_use_of_emoticon,_1862-08-07.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Alleged use of emoticon by <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, in 1862
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Many different forms of communication are now seen as precursors to emoticons and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Emoji" title="Emoji">emoji</a>. The use of emoticons can be traced back to the 17th century, drawn by a <a href="/wiki/Slovaks" title="Slovaks">Slovak</a> notary to indicate his satisfaction with the state of his town's municipal financial records in 1635,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> but they were commonly used in casual and humorous writing. </p>
      <p>The <i>National Telegraphic Review and Operators Guide</i> in April 1857 documented the use of the number 73 in <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">Morse code</a> to express "love and kisses" (later reduced to the more formal "best regards"). <i>Dodge's Manual</i> in 1908 documented the reintroduction of "love and kisses" as the number 88. Gajadhar and Green comment that both <a href="/wiki/Morse_code_abbreviations" title="Morse code abbreviations">Morse code abbreviations</a> are more succinct than modern abbreviations such as <a href="/wiki/LOL_(Internet_slang)" class="mw-redirect" title="LOL (Internet slang)">LOL</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Aside from <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">morse code</a>, other communication tools such as generic <a href="/wiki/Prosign" class="mw-redirect" title="Prosign">prosigns</a> were seen by some as an evolution of language. The first time an emoticon appeared in text was in the transcript of one of <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>'s speeches written in 1862. It contained the following: </p> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942">
      <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
       <p>applause and laughter&nbsp;;) </p> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i>, there has been some debate whether the emoticon in Abraham Lincoln's speech was a <a href="/wiki/Typo" class="mw-redirect" title="Typo">typo</a>, a legitimate punctuation constructs, or the first emoticon.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In the late 1800s, the first emoticons were created as an art form in the U.S. satirical magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)">Puck</a>.</i> In total, four different emoticon designs were displayed, all using punctuation to create different typographical emoticon faces. The emoticon designs were similar to that which formed many years later in Japan, often referred to as <i>"Kaomoji"</i>, due to their complicated design.<sup id="cite_ref-emoticonPuck_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emoticonPuck-14">[14]</a></sup> Despite the innovation, complex emoticons didn't develop in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> until nearly a century later. In 1912, American author <a href="/wiki/Ambrose_Bierce" title="Ambrose Bierce">Ambrose Bierce</a> was the first to suggest that a <a href="/wiki/Bracket" title="Bracket">bracket</a> could be used to represent a smiling face. He stated, "an improvement in punctuation&nbsp;– the snigger point, or note of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cachinnation" class="extiw" title="wikt:cachinnation">cachinnation</a>: it is written thus&nbsp;? and presents a smiling mouth. It is to be appended, with the full stop, to every jocular or ironical sentence".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Emoticons_Puck_1881.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Emoticons_Puck_1881.png/220px-Emoticons_Puck_1881.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="56" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Emoticons_Puck_1881.png/330px-Emoticons_Puck_1881.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Emoticons_Puck_1881.png 2x" data-file-width="434" data-file-height="110"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Emoticons_Puck_1881.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Emoticons published in the March 30, 1881 issue of <i>Puck</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-emoticonPuck_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emoticonPuck-14">[14]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Following this breakthrough statement, other writers and linguistic experts began to put out theories as to how punctuations could be used in collections to represent a face. Moving on from Bierce's theory that a horizontal <a href="/wiki/Bracket" title="Bracket">brackets</a> could be used for a smiling face, Alan Gregg was the first recorded person to suggest that by combining <a href="/wiki/Punctuation_mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Punctuation mark">punctuation marks</a>, more elaborate emotions could be demonstrated. There is an argument that this was the first real set of emoticons, despite later designs becoming the standard for emoticons. Gregg published his theory in 1936, in a <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvard_Lampoon" title="The Harvard Lampoon">Harvard Lampoon</a></i> article. He suggested that by turning the bracket sideways, it could be used for the sides of the mouth or cheeks, with other punctuation used between the brackets to display various emotions. Gregg's theory took the step of creating more than one smiling face, with (-) for a normal smile and (--) for a laughing smile. The logic behind the design was that more teeth were showing on the wider design. Two other emoticons were proposed in the article, with (#) for a frown and (*) for a wink.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Emoticons had already come into use in sci-fi <a href="/wiki/Fandom" title="Fandom">fandom</a> in the 1940s,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> although there seems to have been a lapse in cultural continuity between the communities. </p>
      <p>The September 1962 issue of <a href="/wiki/Mad_(magazine)" title="Mad (magazine)"><i>MAD</i> magazine</a> included an article titled "Typewri-toons". The piece, featuring typewriter-generated artwork credited to "Royal Portable", was entirely made up of repurposed typography, including a capital letter P having a bigger bust than a capital I, a lowercase b and d discussing their pregnancies, an asterisk on top of a letter to indicate the letter had just come inside from snowfall, and a classroom of lowercase n's interrupted by a lowercase h "raising its hand".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Two additional "Typewri-toons" articles subsequently appeared in <i>Mad</i>, in 1965 and 1987. </p>
      <p>A concept for a marker that was similar to Fahlman's concept appeared in an article of <i><a href="/wiki/Reader%27s_Digest" title="Reader's Digest">Reader's Digest</a></i> in May 1967, although that idea was never put into practice.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In a <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> interview in April 1969, <a href="/wiki/Alden_Whitman" title="Alden Whitman">Alden Whitman</a> asked writer <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a>: "How do you rank yourself among writers (living) and of the immediate past?" Nabokov answered: "I often think there should exist a special typographical sign for a smile&nbsp;– some sort of concave mark, a supine round bracket, which I would now like to trace in reply to your question."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Creation_of_:-.29_and_:-.28"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Creation_of_:-)_and_:-(">Creation of&nbsp;:-) and&nbsp;:-(</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Creation of&nbsp;:-) and&nbsp;:-(">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Until this point, many of the designs considered to be early emoticons were created using fairly basic punctuation, using a single <a href="/wiki/Punctuation_mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Punctuation mark">punctuation mark</a> instead of a word or to express feeling, before individuals started combining two punctuations (often a <a href="/wiki/Colon_(punctuation)" title="Colon (punctuation)">colon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bracket" title="Bracket">bracket</a>) to create something that resembled a smiling face.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Scott_Fahlman" title="Scott Fahlman">Scott Fahlman</a> is considered to be the first person to create the first true emoticon as he began to experiment with using multiple <a href="/wiki/Punctuation_mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Punctuation mark">punctuation marks</a> to display emotion and replace language.<sup id="cite_ref-smiley_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smiley-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsteroff1987_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsteroff1987-23">[23]</a></sup> He is the first documented person to use a complex emoticon of three or more punctuation marks, with <code>:-)</code> and <code>:-(</code> with a specific suggestion that they are used to express emotion. Not only did Fahlman create two different emoticons, he also said with the emoticons that they could be used to express emotion. While Nabokov had suggested something similar to Fahlman, there was little analysis of the wider consideration of what Nabokov could do with the design. Fahlman on the other hand quickly theorized that his emoticons could be designed to replace language on a large scale. The two designs of <a href="/wiki/Colon_(punctuation)" title="Colon (punctuation)">colon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyphen" title="Hyphen">hyphen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bracket" title="Bracket">bracket</a> were also adapted very quickly to portray a range of emotions, therefore creating the first true set of emoticons.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The message from Fahlman was sent via the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a> computer science general <a href="/wiki/Bulletin_board_system" title="Bulletin board system">board</a> on September 19, 1982. The conversation was taking place between many notable computer scientists, including <a href="/wiki/David_Touretzky" class="mw-redirect" title="David Touretzky">David Touretzky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guy_L._Steele_Jr." title="Guy L. Steele Jr.">Guy Steele</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jaime_Carbonell" title="Jaime Carbonell">Jaime Carbonell</a>. The messaging transcript was considered to have been lost before it was recovered 20 years later by Jeff Baird from old backup tapes.<sup id="cite_ref-smiley_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smiley-22">[22]</a></sup> </p> 
      <pre style="border: 0px;">19-Sep-82 11:44 Scott E Fahlman            &nbsp;:-)
From: Scott E Fahlman &lt;Fahlman at Cmu-20c&gt;

I propose that the following character sequence for joke markers:

:-)

Read it sideways.  Actually, it is probably more economical to mark
things that are NOT jokes, given current trends.  For this, use

:-(
</pre> 
      <p>Within a few months, it had spread to the <a href="/wiki/ARPANET" title="ARPANET">ARPANET</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Usenet" title="Usenet">Usenet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Many variations on the theme were immediately suggested by Fahlman and others. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_from_emoticons">Evolution from emoticons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Evolution from emoticons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Inspired by <a href="/wiki/Scott_Fahlman" title="Scott Fahlman">Scott Fahlman</a>'s idea of using faces in language, the Loufrani family established <a href="/wiki/The_Smiley_Company" title="The Smiley Company">The Smiley Company</a> in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Nicolas Loufrani developed hundreds of different emoticons, including 3D versions. His designs were registered at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Copyright_Office" title="United States Copyright Office">United States Copyright Office</a> in 1997 and appeared online as .gif files in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-light_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-light-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> These were the first graphical representations of the originally text-based emoticon.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> He published his icons as well as emoticons created by others, along with their ASCII versions, in an online Smiley Dictionary in the early 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-light_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-light-28">[28]</a></sup> This dictionary included over 3,000 different smileys<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and was published as a book called <i>Dico Smileys</i> in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-light_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-light-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-semiotics_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-semiotics-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Fahlman has stated in numerous interviews that he sees <a href="/wiki/Emoji" title="Emoji">emojis</a> as <i>"the remote descendants of this thing I did."</i><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variety_of_styles">Variety of styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Variety of styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western-style">Western-style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Western-style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Usually, emoticons in Western style have the eyes on the left, followed by the nose and the mouth. The two-character version <code>:)</code> which omits the nose is also very popular. </p>
      <p>The most basic emoticons are relatively consistent in form, but each of them can be transformed by being rotated (making them tiny <a href="/wiki/Ambigrams" class="mw-redirect" title="Ambigrams">ambigrams</a>), with or without a hyphen (nose). There are also some possible variations to emoticons to get new definitions, like changing a character to express a new feeling, or slightly change the mood of the emoticon. For example, <code>:(</code> equals sad and <code>:((</code> equals very sad. Weeping can be written as <code>:'(</code>. A blush can be expressed as <code>:"&gt;</code>. Others include wink <code>;)</code>, a grin <code>:D</code>, smug <code>:-&gt;</code>, and can be used to denote a flirting or joking tone, or may be implying a <a href="/wiki/Double_entendre" title="Double entendre">second meaning</a> in the sentence preceding it.<sup id="cite_ref-Dresner_&amp;_Herring_2010_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dresner_&amp;_Herring_2010-35">[35]</a></sup> <code>;P</code>, such as when <a href="/wiki/Blowing_a_raspberry" title="Blowing a raspberry">blowing a raspberry</a>. An often used combination is also <code>&lt;3</code> for a heart, and <code>&lt;/3</code> for a broken heart. <code>:O</code> is also sometimes used to depict shock. <code>:/</code> is used to depict melancholy, disappointment, or disapproval. <code>:|</code> is used to depict a neutral face. </p>
      <p>A broad grin is sometimes shown with crinkled eyes to express further amusement; <code>XD</code> and the addition of further "D" letters can suggest laughter or extreme amusement e.g. <code>XDDDD</code>. The same is true for <code>X3</code> but the three represents an animals mouth. There are other variations including <code>&gt;:(</code> for anger, or <code>&gt;:D</code> for an evil grin, which can be, again, used in reverse, for an unhappy angry face, in the shape of <code>D:&lt;</code>. <code>=K</code> for vampire teeth, <code>:s</code> for grimace, and <code>:P</code> tongue out, can be used to denote a flirting or joking tone, or may be implying a <a href="/wiki/Double_entendre" title="Double entendre">second meaning</a> in the sentence preceding it.<sup id="cite_ref-Dresner_&amp;_Herring_2010_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dresner_&amp;_Herring_2010-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As computers offer increasing built-in support for non-Western writing systems, it has become possible to use other glyphs to build emoticons. The '<a href="/wiki/Shrug" title="Shrug">shrug</a>' emoticon, <code>¯\_(?)_/¯</code>, uses the glyph <a href="/wiki/%E3%83%84" class="mw-redirect" title="?">?</a> from the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> writing system. </p>
      <p>An equal sign is often used for the eyes in place of the colon, seen as <code>=)</code>, without changing the meaning of the emoticon. In these instances, the hyphen is almost always either omitted or, occasionally, replaced with an "o" as in <code>=O)</code>. In most circles it has become acceptable to omit the hyphen, whether a colon or an equal sign is used for the eyes,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> but in some areas of usage people still prefer the larger, more traditional emoticon <code>:-)</code> or <code>:^)</code>. One linguistic study has indicated that the use of a nose in an emoticon may be related to the user's age, with younger people less likely to use a nose.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Similar-looking characters are commonly substituted for one another: for instance, <code>o</code>, <code>O</code>, and <code>0</code> can all be used interchangeably, sometimes for subtly different effect or, in some cases, one type of character may look better in a certain font and therefore be preferred over another. It is also common for the user to replace the rounded brackets used for the mouth with other, similar brackets, such as <code>]</code> instead of <code>)</code>. </p>
      <p>Some variants are also more common in certain countries due to <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_layout" title="Keyboard layout">keyboard layouts</a>. For example, the smiley <code>=)</code> may occur in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, where the keys for <code>=</code> and <code>)</code> are placed right beside each other. However, the <code>:)</code> variant is without a doubt the dominant one in Scandinavia, making the <code>=)</code> version a rarity<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">Diacritical</a> marks are sometimes used. The letters <code>Ö</code> and <code>Ü</code> can be seen as an emoticon, as the upright version of <code>:O</code> (meaning that one is surprised) and <code>:D</code> (meaning that one is very happy) respectively. </p>
      <p>Some emoticons may be read right to left instead, and in fact, can only be written using standard ASCII keyboard characters this way round; for example <code>D:</code> which refers to being shocked or anxious, opposite to the large grin of <code>:D</code>. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Japanese_style_.28kaomoji.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_style_(kaomoji)"><span><span id="Asian_style"><span id="Japanese_style">Japanese style (<i>kaomoji</i>)</span></span></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Japanese style (kaomoji)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:It%27s_all_about_the_smilies_(3578588882).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/It%27s_all_about_the_smilies_%283578588882%29.jpg/180px-It%27s_all_about_the_smilies_%283578588882%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/It%27s_all_about_the_smilies_%283578588882%29.jpg/270px-It%27s_all_about_the_smilies_%283578588882%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/It%27s_all_about_the_smilies_%283578588882%29.jpg/360px-It%27s_all_about_the_smilies_%283578588882%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:It%27s_all_about_the_smilies_(3578588882).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Kaomoji on a Japanese <a href="/wiki/NTT_Docomo" title="NTT Docomo">NTT Docomo</a> mobile phone
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Kaomoji!_(2334722446).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Kaomoji%21_%282334722446%29.jpg/220px-Kaomoji%21_%282334722446%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Kaomoji%21_%282334722446%29.jpg/330px-Kaomoji%21_%282334722446%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Kaomoji%21_%282334722446%29.jpg/440px-Kaomoji%21_%282334722446%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Kaomoji!_(2334722446).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A Kaomoji painting in Japan
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Users from <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> popularized a style of emoticons (<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%A1%94%E6%96%87%E5%AD%97" class="extiw" title="ja:???">???</a>, <i>kaomoji, lit.</i> "face characters") that can be understood without tilting one's head to the left. This style arose on <a href="/wiki/ASCII_Corporation#History" title="ASCII Corporation">ASCII NET</a>, an early Japanese online service, in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-aist_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aist-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-whatjapanthinks2007_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatjapanthinks2007-8">[8]</a></sup> Similar-looking emoticons were used on the <a href="/wiki/Byte_Information_Exchange" title="Byte Information Exchange">Byte Information Exchange</a> (BIX) around the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>These emoticons are usually found in a format similar to <code>(*_*)</code>. The <a href="/wiki/Asterisk" title="Asterisk">asterisks</a> indicate the eyes; the central character, commonly an <a href="/wiki/Underscore" title="Underscore">underscore</a>, the mouth; and the parentheses, the outline of the face. </p>
      <p>Different emotions can be expressed by changing the character representing the eyes: for example, "T" can be used to express crying or sadness: <code>(T_T)</code>. <code>T_T</code> may also be used to mean "unimpressed". The emphasis on the eyes in this style is reflected in the common usage of emoticons that use only the eyes, e.g. <code>^^</code>. Looks of stress are represented by the likes of <code>(x_x)</code>, while <code>(-_-;)</code> is a generic emoticon for nervousness, the semicolon representing an anxiety-induced <a href="/wiki/Sweat_drop" class="mw-redirect" title="Sweat drop">sweat drop</a> (discussed further below). <code>///</code> can indicate embarrassment by symbolizing blushing.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Characters like hyphens or periods can replace the underscore; the period is often used for a smaller, "cuter" mouth, or to represent a nose, e.g. <code>(^.^)</code>. Alternatively, the mouth/nose can be left out entirely, e.g. <code>(^^)</code>. </p>
      <p>Parentheses are sometimes replaced with <a href="/wiki/Bracket#Curly_brackets" title="Bracket">braces</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bracket#Square_brackets" title="Bracket">square brackets</a>, e.g. <code>{^_^}</code> or <code>[o_0]</code>. Many times, the parentheses are left out completely, e.g. <code>^^</code>,<code> &gt;.&lt; </code>, <code>o_O</code>, <code>O.O</code>, <code>e_e</code>, or <code>e.e</code>. A quotation mark <code>"</code>, apostrophe <code>'</code>, or semicolon <code>;</code> can be added to the emoticon to imply apprehension or embarrassment, in the same way that a sweat drop is used in <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a>. </p>
      <p>Microsoft <a href="/wiki/Input_method_editor" class="mw-redirect" title="Input method editor">IME</a> 2000 (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>) or later supports the input of emoticons like the above by enabling the Microsoft IME Spoken Language/Emotion Dictionary. In IME 2007, this support was moved to the Emoticons dictionary. Such dictionaries allow users to call up emoticons by typing words that represent them. </p>
      <p>Communication software allowing the use of <a href="/wiki/Shift_JIS" title="Shift JIS">Shift JIS</a> encoded characters rather than just ASCII allowed for the development of more kaomoji using the extended character set including <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a>, symbols, <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabets" title="Cyrillic alphabets">Cyrillic alphabet</a>, such as <code><span class="nowrap">(^?^)</span></code>, <code>(`?´)</code> or <code>(?)</code>. </p>
      <p>Modern communication software generally utilizes <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>, which allows for the incorporation of characters from other languages and a variety of symbols into the kaomoji, as in <code>(????)</code>. </p>
      <p>Further variations can be produced using Unicode <a href="/wiki/Combining_character" title="Combining character">combining characters</a>, as in <code>?(?????)?</code> or <code>?????</code>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combination_of_Japanese_and_Western_styles">Combination of Japanese and Western styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Combination of Japanese and Western styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>English-language <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> forums adopted those Japanese-style emoticons that could be used with the standard ASCII characters available on Western keyboards. Because of this, they are often called "anime style" emoticons in English<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. They have since seen use in more mainstream venues, including online gaming, instant-messaging, and non-anime-related discussion forums. Emoticons such as <code>&lt;(&nbsp;^.^&nbsp;)&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;(^_^&lt;)</code>, <code>&lt;(o_o&lt;)</code>, <code>&lt;(&nbsp;-'.'-&nbsp;)&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;('.'-^)</code>, or <code>(&gt;';..;')&gt;</code> which include the parentheses, mouth or nose, and arms (especially those represented by the inequality signs &lt; or &gt;) also are often referred to as "Kirbys" in reference to their likeness to <a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video game</a> character <a href="/wiki/Kirby_(Nintendo)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kirby (Nintendo)">Kirby</a>. The parentheses are sometimes dropped when used in the English language context, and the underscore of the mouth may be extended as an <a href="/wiki/Intensifier" title="Intensifier">intensifier</a> for the emoticon in question, e.g. <code>^_________^</code> for very happy. The emoticon <span class="nowrap"><code>t(-_-t)</code></span> uses the Eastern style, but incorporates a depiction of the Western <a href="/wiki/The_finger" title="The finger">"middle-finger flick-off"</a> using a "t" as the arm, hand, and finger. Using a <a href="/wiki/Lateral_click" title="Lateral click">lateral click</a> for the nose such as in <span style="background-color: #f8f9fa; border: 1px solid #eaecf0; padding: 1px; text-align: center; vertical-align: bottom"><span class="nowrap">( ?° ?? ?°)</span></span> is believed to originate from the Finnish image-based message board Ylilauta, and is called a "Lenny face".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Another apparently Western invention is the use of emoticons like <code>*,..,*</code> or <code>`;..;´</code> to indicate vampires or other mythical beasts with fangs. </p>
      <p><span class="anchor" id="OwO"></span><span class="anchor" id="UwU"></span>Exposure to both Western and Japanese style emoticons or kaomoji through blogs, instant messaging, and forums featuring a blend of Western and Japanese pop culture has given rise to many emoticons that have an upright viewing format. The parentheses are often dropped, and these emoticons typically only use alphanumeric characters and the most commonly used English punctuation marks. Emoticons such as <code>-O-</code>, <code>-3-</code>, <code>-w-</code>, <code>'_'</code>, <code>;_;</code>, <code>T_T</code>, <code>:&gt;</code>, and <code>.V.</code> are used to convey mixed emotions that are more difficult to convey with traditional emoticons. Characters are sometimes added to emoticons to convey an anime- or manga-styled sweat drop, for example <code>^_^'</code>, <code>!&gt;_&lt;!</code>, <code>&lt;@&gt;_____&lt;@&gt;;;</code>, <code>;O;</code>, and <code>*u*</code>. The equals sign can also be used for closed, anime-looking eyes, for example <code>=0=</code>, <code>=3=</code>, <code>=w=</code>, <code>=A=</code>, and <code>=7=</code>. The <code><a href="/wiki/Uwu_(emoticon)" title="Uwu (emoticon)">uwu</a></code> face (and its variations <code>UwU</code> and <code>OwO</code>), is an emoticon of Japanese origin which denotes a cute expression or emotion felt by the user.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Brazil, sometimes combining characters (accents) are added to emoticons to represent eyebrows, as in <code>ò_ó</code>, <code>ó_ò</code>, <code>õ_o</code>, <code>ù_u</code>, or <code>o_Ô</code>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2channel_style">2channel style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 2channel style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Users of the Japanese discussion board <a href="/wiki/2channel" title="2channel">2channel</a>, in particular, have developed a wide variety of unique emoticons using characters from various scripts, such as <a href="/wiki/Kannada_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Kannada alphabet">Kannada</a>, as in <code>?_?</code> (for a look of disapproval, disbelief, or confusion). These were quickly picked up by <a href="/wiki/4chan" title="4chan">4chan</a> and spread to other Western sites soon after. Some have taken on a life of their own and become characters in their own right, like <a href="/wiki/Mona_(ASCII_art)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mona (ASCII art)">Mon?</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_style">Korean style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Korean style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, emoticons use Korean <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a> letters, and the Western style is rarely used.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The structures of Korean and Japanese emoticons are somewhat similar, but they have some differences. Korean style contains Korean <a href="/wiki/Hangul_consonant_and_vowel_tables" title="Hangul consonant and vowel tables">jamo</a> (letters) instead of other characters. There are countless number of emoticons that can be formed with such combinations of Korean jamo letters. Consonant jamos <code>?</code>, <code>?</code> or <code>?</code> as the mouth/nose component and <code>?</code>, <code>?</code> or <code>?</code> for the eyes. For example: <code>???</code>, <code>???</code>, <code>???</code> and <code>-?-</code>. Faces such as <code>'?'</code>, <code>"?"</code>, <code>'?'</code> and <code>'?'</code>, using quotation marks <code>"</code> and apostrophes <code>'</code> are also commonly used combinations. Vowel jamos such as ?,? depict a crying face. Example: <code>??</code>, <code>??</code> and <code>??</code> (same function as T in western style). Sometimes ? (not an em-dash "—" but a vowel jamo), a comma or an underscore is added, and the two character sets can be mixed together, as in <code>?.?</code>, <code>?.?</code>, <code>?.?</code>, <code>?_?</code>, <code>?^?</code> and <code>???</code>. Also, semicolons and carets are commonly used in Korean emoticons; semicolons mean sweating (embarrassed). If they are used with ? or – they depict a bad feeling. Examples: <code>-;/</code>, <code>--^</code>, <code>??;;;</code>, <code>-_-;;</code> and <code>-_^</code>. However, <code>^^, ^?^</code> means smile (almost all people use this without distinction of sex or age). Others include: <code>~_~</code>, <code>--a</code>, <code>-6-</code>, <code>+0+</code>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_ideographic_style">Chinese ideographic style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chinese ideographic style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Jiong" title="Jiong">Jiong</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The character ? (U+56E7), which means "bright", may be combined with posture emoticon Orz, such as ?rz. The character existed in <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">Oracle bone script</a>, but its use as emoticon was documented as early as January 20, 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-nownews-56E7_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nownews-56E7-44">[44]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Other ideographic variants for ? include ? (king ?), ? (queen ?), ? (? with hat), ?? (turtle), ? (<a href="/wiki/Bomberman" title="Bomberman">Bomberman</a>). </p>
      <p>The character ? (U+69D1), which sounds like the word for "plum" (? (U+FA44)), is used to represent double of ? (dull), or further magnitude of dullness. In Chinese, normally full characters (as opposed to the stylistic use of ?) might be <a href="/wiki/Reduplication#Chinese" title="Reduplication">duplicated</a> to express emphasis. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_smiley">Russian smiley</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Russian smiley">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>On the Russian-speaking Internet, the right parenthesis <code>)</code> is used as a smiley. Multiple parentheses <code>))))</code> are used to express greater happiness, amusement or laughter. It is commonly placed at the end of a sentence. The colon is omitted due to being in a lesser-known position on the <a href="/wiki/JCUKEN" title="JCUKEN">?????? keyboard layout</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Posture_emoticons">Posture emoticons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Posture emoticons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orz">Orz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Orz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Dogeza_2015-01-15.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Dogeza_2015-01-15.png/220px-Dogeza_2015-01-15.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="470" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Dogeza_2015-01-15.png/330px-Dogeza_2015-01-15.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/Dogeza_2015-01-15.png 2x" data-file-width="415" data-file-height="887"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Dogeza_2015-01-15.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Japanese custom of <i><a href="/wiki/Dogeza" title="Dogeza">dogeza</a></i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Orz" class="extiw" title="wikt:Orz">Orz</a> (other forms include: <span class="nowrap">Or2,</span> <span class="nowrap">on_,</span> <span class="nowrap">OTZ,</span> <span class="nowrap">OTL,</span> <span class="nowrap">STO,</span> <span class="nowrap">JTO,</span><sup id="cite_ref-boing-orz_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boing-orz-45">[45]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap">_no,</span> <span class="nowrap">???,</span><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Jiong" title="Jiong">?</a>?rz,</span><sup id="cite_ref-nownews-56E7_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nownews-56E7-44">[44]</a></sup>) is an emoticon representing a kneeling or bowing person (the Japanese version of which is called <i><a href="/wiki/Dogeza" title="Dogeza">dogeza</a></i>) with the "o" being the head, the "r" being the arms and part of the body, and the "z" being part of the body and the legs. This <a href="/wiki/Stick_figure" title="Stick figure">stick figure</a> can represent respect or <i>kowtowing</i>, but commonly appears along a range of responses, including "frustration, despair, sarcasm, or grudging respect".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It was first used in late 2002 at the forum on Techside, a Japanese personal website. At the "Techside FAQ Forum" (TECHSIDE????BBS(???BBS) ), a poster asked about a cable cover, typing <span class="nowrap">"?|?|?"</span> to show a cable and its cover. Others commented that it looked like a kneeling person, and the symbol became popular.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> These comments were soon deleted as they were considered off-topic. By 2005, Orz spawned a <a href="/wiki/Subculture" title="Subculture">subculture</a>: blogs have been devoted to the emoticon, and <a href="/wiki/URL_shortening" title="URL shortening">URL shortening</a> services have been named after it. In Taiwan, Orz is associated with the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Nice_guy" title="Nice guy">nice guy</a>"&nbsp;– that is, the concept of males being rejected for a date by females, with a phrase like "You are a nice guy."<sup id="cite_ref-boing-orz_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boing-orz-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Orz should not be (???) confused with <b><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/m(_)m" class="extiw" title="wikt:m( )m">m(_&nbsp;_)m</a></b>, which means "Thank you" or an apology (? ?? ??? ?? ).<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Multimedia_variations">Multimedia variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Multimedia variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>A portmanteau of <i>emotion</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a></i>, an <b>emotisound</b> is a brief sound transmitted and played back during the viewing of a message, typically an IM message or e-mail message. The sound is intended to communicate an emotional <a href="/wiki/Subtext" title="Subtext">subtext</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Journal_of_effectus_for_college_of_law_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Journal_of_effectus_for_college_of_law-50">[50]</a></sup> Many instant messaging clients automatically trigger sound effects in response to specific emoticons.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Some services, such as MuzIcons, combine emoticons and music player in an <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash" title="Adobe Flash">Adobe Flash</a>-based widget.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Trillian_(software)" title="Trillian (software)">Trillian</a> chat application introduced a feature called "emotiblips", which allows Trillian users to stream files to their instant message recipients "as the voice and video equivalent of an emoticon".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2007, <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures_Home_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Paramount Pictures Home Entertainment">Paramount Home Entertainment</a> promoted the "emoticlip" as a form of <a href="/wiki/Viral_marketing" title="Viral marketing">viral marketing</a> for the second season of the show <i>The Hills</i>. The emoticlips were twelve short snippets of dialogue from the show, uploaded to YouTube, which the advertisers hoped would be distributed between web users as a way of expressing feelings in a similar manner to emoticons. The emoticlip concept is credited to the Bradley &amp; Montgomery advertising firm, which hopes they would be widely adopted as "greeting cards that just happen to be selling something".<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2008, an emotion-sequence animation tool, called FunIcons was created. The <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash" title="Adobe Flash">Adobe Flash</a> and <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>-based application allows users to create a short animation. Users can then email or save their own animations to use them on similar social utility applications.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the first half of the 2010s, there have been different forms of small audiovisual pieces to be sent through instant messaging systems to express one's emotion. These videos lack an established name, and there are several ways to designate them: "emoticlips" (named above), "emotivideos" or more recently "emoticon videos". These are tiny videos that can be easily transferred from one mobile phone to another. Current video compression codecs such as <a href="/wiki/H.264" class="mw-redirect" title="H.264">H.264</a> allow these pieces of video to be light in terms of file size and very portable. The popular computer and mobile app <a href="/wiki/Skype" title="Skype">Skype</a> use these in a separate keyboard or by typing the code of the "emoticon videos" between parentheses. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emoticons_and_intellectual_property_rights">Emoticons and intellectual property rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Emoticons and intellectual property rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:USpatent6987991_emoticon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/USpatent6987991_emoticon.jpg/220px-USpatent6987991_emoticon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/USpatent6987991_emoticon.jpg/330px-USpatent6987991_emoticon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/USpatent6987991_emoticon.jpg/440px-USpatent6987991_emoticon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="571" data-file-height="559"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:USpatent6987991_emoticon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Patented drop down menu for composing phone mail text message with emoticons. <span class="citation patent"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://worldwide.espacenet.com/textdoc?DB=EPODOC&amp;IDX=US6987991">US 6987991</a></span><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Apatent&amp;rft.number=6987991&amp;rft.cc=US&amp;rft.title="><span style="display: none;">&nbsp;</span></span>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2000, <a href="/wiki/Despair,_Inc." title="Despair, Inc.">Despair, Inc.</a> obtained a U.S. <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademark</a> registration for the "frowny" emoticon <code>:-(</code> when used on "greeting cards, posters and art prints". In 2001, they issued a satirical press release, announcing that they would sue Internet users who typed the frowny; the joke backfired and the company received a storm of protest when its mock release was posted on technology news website <a href="/wiki/Slashdot" title="Slashdot">Slashdot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A number of <a href="/wiki/Patent_application" title="Patent application">patent applications</a> have been filed on inventions that assist in communicating with emoticons. A few of these have been issued as US <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patents</a>. <span class="citation patent"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://worldwide.espacenet.com/textdoc?DB=EPODOC&amp;IDX=US6987991">US 6987991</a></span><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Apatent&amp;rft.number=6987991&amp;rft.cc=US&amp;rft.title="><span style="display: none;">&nbsp;</span></span>, for example, discloses a method developed in 2001 to send emoticons over a cell phone using a drop-down menu. The stated advantage over the <a href="/wiki/Prior_art" title="Prior art">prior art</a> was that the user saved on the number of keystrokes though this may not address the obviousness criteria. </p>
      <p>The emoticon <code>:-)</code> was also filed in 2006 and registered in 2008 as a European <a href="/wiki/Community_Trademark" class="mw-redirect" title="Community Trademark">Community Trademark</a> (CTM). In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, the Supreme Administrative Court ruled in 2012 that the emoticon cannot be trademarked,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> thus repealing a 2006 administrative decision trademarking the emoticons <code>:-)</code>, <code>=)</code>, <code>=(</code>, <code>:)</code> and <code>:(</code>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2005, a Russian court rejected a legal claim against <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> by a man who claimed to hold a trademark on the <code>;-)</code> emoticon.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcwink_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcwink-58">[58]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2008, Russian entrepreneur Oleg Teterin claimed to have been granted the trademark on the <code>;-)</code> emoticon. A license would not "cost that much&nbsp;– tens of thousands of dollars" for companies, but would be free of charge for individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcwink_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcwink-58">[58]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Unicode">Unicode</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emoticon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Unicode">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Emoticons_(Unicode_block)" title="Emoticons (Unicode block)">Emoticons (Unicode block)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>A different, but related, use of the term "emoticon" is found in the <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicode Standard">Unicode Standard</a>, referring to a subset of <a href="/wiki/Emoji" title="Emoji">emoji</a> which display facial expressions.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The standard explains this usage with reference to existing systems, which provided functionality for substituting certain textual emoticons with images or emoji of the expressions in question.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some smiley faces were present in Unicode since <a href="/wiki/Unicode_1.1" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicode 1.1">1.1</a>, including a white <a href="/wiki/Frowning" class="mw-redirect" title="Frowning">frowning</a> face, a white <a href="/wiki/Smiling" class="mw-redirect" title="Smiling">smiling</a> face, and a black smiling face. ("Black" refers to a glyph which is filled, "white" refers to a glyph which is unfilled).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>

         <td colspan="17" style="background:#F8F8F8;font-size:small;text-align:left"><b>Notes</b> 
          <dl>
           <dd>
            1.<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_U2600_as_of_Unicode_version"><b><a href="#ref_U2600_as_of_Unicode_version">^</a></b></span> As of Unicode version 13.0
           </dd> 
           <dd>
            2.<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_U2600_white"><b><a href="#ref_U2600_white">^</a></b></span> Empty areas indicate code points assigned to non-emoticon characters
           </dd> 
           <dd>
            3.<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_U263A_U263B"><b><a href="#ref_U263A_U263B">^</a></b></span> U+263A and U+263B are inherited from Microsoft <a href="/wiki/Code_page_437" title="Code page 437">code page 437</a> introduced in 1981, although inspired by older systems
           </dd>
          </dl> </td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Emoticons_(Unicode_block)" title="Emoticons (Unicode block)">Emoticons</a> block was introduced in <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> Standard <a href="/wiki/Unicode_6.0" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicode 6.0">version 6.0</a> (published in October 2010) and extended by <a href="/wiki/Unicode_7.0" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicode 7.0">7.0</a>. It covers Unicode range from U+1F600 to U+1F64F fully.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> </p>

         <td colspan="17" style="background:#F8F8F8;font-size:small;text-align:left"><b>Notes</b> 
          <dl>
           <dd>
            1.<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_U1F600_as_of_Unicode_version"><b><a href="#ref_U1F600_as_of_Unicode_version">^</a></b></span> As of Unicode version 13.0
           </dd>

      <p>After that block had been filled, <a href="/wiki/Unicode_8.0" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicode 8.0">Unicode 8.0</a> (2015), <a href="/wiki/Unicode_9.0" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicode 9.0">9.0</a> (2016) and <a href="/wiki/Unicode_10.0" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicode 10.0">10.0</a> (2017) added additional emoticons in the range from U+1F910 to U+1F9FF. Currently, U+1F90C&nbsp;– U+1F90F, U+1F93F, U+1F94D&nbsp;– U+1F94F, U+1F96C&nbsp;– U+1F97F, U+1F998&nbsp;– U+1F9CF (excluding U+1F9C0 which contains the <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">?</a> emoji) and U+1F9E7&nbsp;– U+1F9FF do not contain any emoticons since Unicode 10.0. </p>

            1.<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_U1F900_as_of_Unicode_version"><b><a href="#ref_U1F900_as_of_Unicode_version">^</a></b></span> As of Unicode version 13.0

      <p>For historic and compatibility reasons, some other heads, and figures, which mostly represent different aspects like genders, activities, and professions instead of emotions, are also found in <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols_and_Pictographs#Table_of_emojis_with_modifiers" title="Miscellaneous Symbols and Pictographs">Miscellaneous Symbols and Pictographs</a> (especially U+1F466&nbsp;– U+1F487) and <a href="/wiki/Transport_and_Map_Symbols#Diversity" title="Transport and Map Symbols">Transport and Map Symbols</a>. Body parts, mostly hands, are also encoded in the <a href="/wiki/Dingbat#Skin_tones" title="Dingbat">Dingbat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols#Diversity" title="Miscellaneous Symbols">Miscellaneous Symbols</a> blocks. </p>

       <li>Bódi, Zoltán, and Veszelszki, Ágnes (2006). <i>Emotikonok. Érzelemkifejezés az internetes kommunikációban</i> (Emoticons. Expressing emotions in the internet communication). Budapest: Magyar Szemiotikai Társaság.</li>
